--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that if `w` is a non-empty word and `n > 0`, then `w ^ n` is non-empty.

Let me think about the structure:
- We have `w ≠ []` (non-empty word)
- We have `n : ℕ` with `0 < n` (positive natural number)
- We want to show `w ^ n ≠ []`

From the CoW library, I can see that:
1. `Word.pow_zero` gives us `w ^ 0 = Word.empty = []`
2. `Word.pow_succ` gives us `w ^ (n + 1) = w ^ n ⬝ w`
3. `Word.length_pow` gives us `(w ^ n).length = n * w.length`

The key insight is to use the length formula. If `w ≠ []`, then `w.length > 0`. Since `n > 0`, we have `n * w.length > 0`, which means `(w ^ n).length > 0`, which implies `w ^ n ≠ []`.

Let me check what lemmas are available for this approach:
- I need `w.length > 0` from `w ≠ []`. Looking at the library, I can use `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` which gives `0 < w.length ↔ w ≠ []`.
- I have `Word.length_pow` which gives `(w ^ n).length = n * w.length`
- I need to show that if `(w ^ n).length > 0` then `w ^ n ≠ []`. This should follow from `List.length_eq_zero` which gives `w.length = 0 ↔ w = []`, so the contrapositive gives `w ≠ [] ↔ w.length ≠ 0`.

Actually, let me be more precise about `List.length_eq_zero`. From Mathlib, this should give us `List.length w = 0 ↔ w = []`, so the contrapositive is `w ≠ [] ↔ List.length w ≠ 0`.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this theorem using a length argument combined with properties of word powers.

**Step 1:** Convert the hypothesis `w ≠ []` to a length condition
- Use `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` to get `0 < w.length` from `w ≠ []`

**Step 2:** Apply the length formula for word powers  
- Use `Word.length_pow` to get `(w ^ n).length = n * w.length`

**Step 3:** Show the product is positive
- Since `0 < n` (given) and `0 < w.length` (from Step 1), use arithmetic to conclude `0 < n * w.length`
- This gives us `0 < (w ^ n).length`

**Step 4:** Convert back to non-emptiness
- Use the contrapositive of `List.length_eq_zero` (which states `w.length = 0 ↔ w = []`) to conclude that if `0 < (w ^ n).length`, then `w ^ n ≠ []`

The key lemmas I'll reference are:
- `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` (from Mathlib)
- `Word.length_pow` (from CoW.Word)
- `List.length_eq_zero` (from Mathlib) 
- Basic arithmetic facts about natural number multiplication

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that `u ⬝ v = [] ↔ u = [] ∧ v = []`, which is a biconditional about when the concatenation of two words equals the empty word.

Let me think about the structure:
- `u ⬝ v = []` means the concatenation is empty
- This should happen if and only if both `u = []` and `v = []`

From the CoW library, I can see that:
1. `Word.concat` is defined as `List.append` (i.e., `u ⬝ v = u ++ v`)
2. There should be a corresponding lemma in Mathlib for lists

Looking at standard list theory, the key insight is that `List.append u v = []` if and only if both `u = []` and `v = []`. This is because:
- If either `u` or `v` is non-empty, then `u ++ v` will have positive length
- Only when both are empty does the concatenation become empty

I need to find the appropriate Mathlib lemma. This should be something like `List.append_eq_nil_iff` or similar.

The proof structure should be:
1. Use the fact that `Word.concat` is `List.append`
2. Apply the Mathlib lemma about when `List.append` equals `nil`
3. The result should be immediate

Let me also check what's available in the CoW library itself. I see that `Word.concat` is an abbreviation for `List.append`, so I can work directly with the List.append properties.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this biconditional by using the underlying List.append properties, since Word.concat is defined as List.append.

**Step 1:** Unfold the definition of Word.concat
- Since `u ⬝ v` is defined as `List.append u v`, I can rewrite the goal to `u ++ v = [] ↔ u = [] ∧ v = []`

**Step 2:** Apply the Mathlib lemma for List.append
- Use `List.append_eq_nil_iff` (or similar lemma from Mathlib) which should state that `List.append u v = [] ↔ u = [] ∧ v = []`

**Step 3:** The proof should be immediate
- Since Word α is just List α, the lemma should apply directly

The key lemma I expect to use:
- `List.append_eq_nil_iff` (from Mathlib's Data.List.Basic)

If this exact lemma name doesn't exist, I may need to:
- Look for alternative names like `List.nil_iff_append` or similar
- Or prove it manually using length arguments with `List.length_append` and the fact that lengths are non-negative

--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that `w ^ n = [] ↔ (n = 0 ∨ w = [])`, which is a biconditional about when a word power equals the empty word.

Let me think about the structure:
- `w ^ n = []` means the n-th power is empty
- This should happen if and only if either `n = 0` (since any word to the 0th power is empty) or `w = []` (since the empty word to any power is empty)

From the CoW library, I can see that:
1. `Word.pow_zero` gives us `w ^ 0 = Word.empty = []`
2. `Word.one_pow` gives us `([] : Word α) ^ n = []`
3. `Word.pow_succ` gives us `w ^ (n + 1) = w ^ n ⬝ w`
4. `Word.length_pow` gives us `(w ^ n).length = n * w.length`

The proof strategy should be to split into two directions:

**Forward direction** (`w ^ n = [] → n = 0 ∨ w = []`):
- If `n = 0`, we're done
- If `n > 0`, I need to show `w = []`
- I can use a length argument: if `w ^ n = []`, then `(w ^ n).length = 0`
- By `Word.length_pow`, we have `n * w.length = 0`
- Since `n > 0`, we must have `w.length = 0`, which means `w = []`

**Backward direction** (`n = 0 ∨ w = [] → w ^ n = []`):
- Case 1: If `n = 0`, use `Word.pow_zero`
- Case 2: If `w = []`, use `Word.one_pow` (which gives `[] ^ n = []`)

Let me check what lemmas I need:
- `List.length_eq_zero` for converting between `w.length = 0` and `w = []`
- Basic arithmetic facts about when `n * w.length = 0`

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this biconditional by splitting into forward and backward directions, using length arguments and the power lemmas from the CoW library.

**Step 1:** Split the biconditional into two directions

**Forward direction** (`w ^ n = [] → n = 0 ∨ w = []`):

**Step 2:** Use case analysis on whether `n = 0`
- If `n = 0`, then the conclusion `n = 0 ∨ w = []` is immediate

**Step 3:** For the case `n > 0`, prove `w = []`
- Assume `w ^ n = []` and `n > 0`
- Use `Word.length_pow` to get `(w ^ n).length = n * w.length`
- Since `w ^ n = []`, we have `(w ^ n).length = 0`
- Therefore `n * w.length = 0`
- Since `n > 0`, we must have `w.length = 0`
- Use `List.length_eq_zero` to conclude `w = []`

**Backward direction** (`n = 0 ∨ w = [] → w ^ n = []`):

**Step 4:** Case analysis on the disjunction
- Case 1: If `n = 0`, use `Word.pow_zero` to get `w ^ 0 = []`
- Case 2: If `w = []`, use `Word.one_pow` to get `[] ^ n = []`

**Key lemmas I'll reference:**
- `Word.pow_zero` (from CoW.Word)
- `Word.one_pow` (from CoW.Word) 
- `Word.length_pow` (from CoW.Word)
- `List.length_eq_zero` (from Mathlib)
- Basic arithmetic about when products equal zero

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that `w ^ m ⬝ w ^ n = w ^ n ⬝ w ^ m`, which states that powers of the same word commute under concatenation.

Let me think about this mathematically. In the monoid of words under concatenation, we have:
- `w ^ m ⬝ w ^ n = w ^ m * w ^ n` (since `⬝` is the same as `*`)
- By the power addition law: `w ^ m * w ^ n = w ^ (m + n)`
- Similarly: `w ^ n * w ^ m = w ^ (n + m)`
- Since addition of natural numbers is commutative: `m + n = n + m`
- Therefore: `w ^ (m + n) = w ^ (n + m)`

From the CoW library, I can see:
1. `Word.mul_eq_concat` gives us `u * v = u ⬝ v`
2. `Word.pow_add` gives us `w ^ (m + n) = w ^ m ⬝ w ^ n`
3. Addition commutativity `Nat.add_comm` gives us `m + n = n + m`

The proof strategy is straightforward:
1. Convert concatenation to multiplication
2. Use the power addition law in both directions
3. Apply commutativity of natural number addition

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this by using the power addition law and commutativity of natural number addition.

**Step 1:** Convert concatenations to multiplications
- Use `Word.mul_eq_concat` to rewrite both sides as `w ^ m * w ^ n = w ^ n * w ^ m`

**Step 2:** Apply power addition law on the left side
- Use `Word.pow_add` in the reverse direction to get `w ^ m * w ^ n = w ^ (m + n)`

**Step 3:** Apply power addition law on the right side  
- Use `Word.pow_add` in the reverse direction to get `w ^ n * w ^ m = w ^ (n + m)`

**Step 4:** Use commutativity of addition
- Apply `Nat.add_comm` to show `m + n = n + m`
- This gives us `w ^ (m + n) = w ^ (n + m)`

**Step 5:** Conclude the equality
- The equation follows from the transitivity of equality

**Key lemmas I'll reference:**
- `Word.mul_eq_concat` (from CoW.Word) - to convert `⬝` to `*`
- `Word.pow_add` (from CoW.Word) - states `w ^ (m + n) = w ^ m ⬝ w ^ n`
- `Nat.add_comm` (from Mathlib) - commutativity of natural number addition

**Alternative approach:** I could work entirely with concatenation notation using the fact that `Word.pow_add` already uses `⬝` notation, avoiding the need to convert to multiplication.

--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that if `u` is a factor of `w`, then `u` is also a factor of `w ⬝ v` for any word `v`. This makes intuitive sense: if `u` appears as a contiguous subword in `w`, then it will still appear as a contiguous subword when we append `v` to `w`.

From the CoW library, I can see that:
1. `IsFactor` is defined as: `u ≤f w` iff `∃ x y : Word α, w = x ++ u ++ y`
2. I need to show that `u ≤f (w ⬝ v)`, which means `∃ x' y' : Word α, (w ⬝ v) = x' ++ u ++ y'`
3. `Word.concat` is defined as `List.append`, so `w ⬝ v = w ++ v`

The proof strategy:
1. Since `u ≤f w`, there exist words `x` and `y` such that `w = x ++ u ++ y`
2. I want to show `u ≤f (w ⬝ v)`, which means finding `x'` and `y'` such that `w ⬝ v = x' ++ u ++ y'`
3. I can substitute: `w ⬝ v = (x ++ u ++ y) ++ v = x ++ u ++ (y ++ v)` by associativity of append
4. So I can take `x' = x` and `y' = y ++ v`

This uses the associativity of list append, which should be available in Mathlib.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this by using the definition of factors and the associativity of list concatenation.

**Step 1:** Unfold the hypothesis using the factor definition
- From `u ≤f w`, obtain witnesses `x` and `y` such that `w = x ++ u ++ y`

**Step 2:** Set up the goal using the factor definition  
- Need to show `u ≤f (w ⬝ v)`, which means finding `x'` and `y'` such that `(w ⬝ v) = x' ++ u ++ y'`

**Step 3:** Substitute the decomposition of `w`
- Since `w ⬝ v = w ++ v` and `w = x ++ u ++ y`, we have:
- `w ⬝ v = (x ++ u ++ y) ++ v`

**Step 4:** Use associativity to rearrange
- Apply `List.append_assoc` to get `(x ++ u ++ y) ++ v = x ++ u ++ (y ++ v)`

**Step 5:** Provide the witnesses
- Take `x' = x` and `y' = y ++ v`  
- Then `w ⬝ v = x' ++ u ++ y'`, which shows `u ≤f (w ⬝ v)`

**Key lemmas I'll reference:**
- Definition of `IsFactor` (from CoW.Factor) - `u ≤f w ↔ ∃ x y, w = x ++ u ++ y`
- `Word.concat` definition (from CoW.Word) - `w ⬝ v = w ++ v`  
- `List.append_assoc` (from Mathlib) - associativity of list append

**Alternative approach:** I could also use `IsFactor.trans` if there's a way to show that concatenating on the right preserves the factor relation, but the direct approach seems more straightforward.

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that if `u` is a prefix of `w`, `v` is a suffix of `w`, and their lengths sum to `w`'s length, then `w = u ⬝ v`.

This is a fundamental theorem about how prefixes and suffixes can partition a word when their lengths are complementary.

From the CoW library:
1. `IsPrefix.def` gives us: `u ≤ₚ w ↔ ∃ suffix, w = u ++ suffix`
2. `IsSuffix.def` gives us: `v ≤ₛ w ↔ ∃ prefix, w = prefix ++ v`
3. The length constraint `u.length + v.length = w.length` is crucial

The key insight is that if `u` is a prefix and `v` is a suffix with complementary lengths, then they must "meet exactly" in the middle without overlap or gap.

Here's the strategy:
1. From `hpre`, get some `s` such that `w = u ++ s`
2. From `hsuf`, get some `p` such that `w = p ++ v`  
3. Use the length constraint to show that `s = v` and `p = u`

For step 3, I can use:
- From `w = u ++ s`, we have `w.length = u.length + s.length`
- From the constraint `u.length + v.length = w.length`, we get `s.length = v.length`
- From `w = p ++ v`, we have `w.length = p.length + v.length`
- From the constraint, we get `p.length = u.length`
- Since `w = u ++ s = p ++ v` and the lengths match up, we should have `u = p` and `s = v`

For the last step, I need a lemma about list append being injective when lengths are determined. This might be something like list extensionality or a cancellation property.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this by using the prefix and suffix decompositions along with the length constraint to show that the word can be uniquely partitioned.

**Step 1:** Extract the prefix and suffix decompositions
- From `hpre : u ≤ₚ w`, use `IsPrefix.def` to get `s` such that `w = u ++ s`
- From `hsuf : v ≤ₛ w`, use `IsSuffix.def` to get `p` such that `w = p ++ v`

**Step 2:** Establish length relationships
- From `w = u ++ s`, we have `w.length = u.length + s.length` (by `List.length_append`)
- From `w = p ++ v`, we have `w.length = p.length + v.length` (by `List.length_append`)
- From the constraint `hlen : u.length + v.length = w.length`

**Step 3:** Use the length constraint to determine the intermediate pieces
- From `w.length = u.length + s.length` and `w.length = u.length + v.length`, conclude `s.length = v.length`
- From `w.length = p.length + v.length` and `w.length = u.length + v.length`, conclude `p.length = u.length`

**Step 4:** Show that the pieces are equal using the append equations
- Since `w = u ++ s = p ++ v` and we know the lengths are equal (`u.length = p.length` and `s.length = v.length`), use a list lemma to conclude `u = p` and `s = v`

**Step 5:** Conclude the main result
- Since `s = v`, we have `w = u ++ s = u ++ v = u ⬝ v`

- Since `u` is a prefix, `w.take u.length = u`
- This should follow from the prefix definition and properties of `List.take`

**Step 4:** Show that the dropped part equals `v`  
- From the length constraint `u.length + v.length = w.length`, we get `w.length - v.length = u.length`
- Since `v` is a suffix, `w.drop (w.length - v.length) = v`
- Therefore `w.drop u.length = v`

**Step 5:** Combine the results
- From steps 2, 3, and 4: `w = u ++ v`
- Convert to concatenation notation: `w = u ⬝ v`

**Key lemmas I'll reference:**
- `IsPrefix.def` and `IsSuffix.def` (from CoW.Factor)
- `List.take_append_drop` (from Mathlib) - states `l = l.take n ++ l.drop n`
- `List.take_prefix` or similar (from Mathlib) - relating prefixes to `take`
- `List.drop_suffix` or similar (from Mathlib) - relating suffixes to `drop`
- Basic arithmetic about the length constraint

**Alternative approach:** If the take/drop lemmas aren't readily available, I could prove this by induction on the structure of `u`, showing element-wise equality.

```lean4
-- Extract the basic decompositions
obtain ⟨s, hs⟩ := (IsPrefix.def u w).mp hpre
obtain ⟨p, hp⟩ := (IsSuffix.def v w).mp hsuf

-- Establish that p.length = u.length using the length constraint

  have h1 : w.length = u.length + s.length := by
    rw [hs]; exact List.length_append u s
  have h2 : w.length = p.length + v.length := by
    rw [hp]; exact List.length_append p v
  omega

-- Since p and u have the same length and both start w when combined with their suffixes,
-- and we have the exact length constraint, we must have p = u and s = v
have h_eq : u ++ s = p ++ v := by
  rw [← hs, ← hp]

-- Use list extensionality: since lengths match, the prefixes must be equal
have h_u_eq_p : u = p := by
  have h_take_u : (u ++ s).take u.length = u := List.take_left u s
  have h_take_p : (p ++ v).take p.length = p := List.take_left p v
  rw [h_p_len] at h_take_p
  rw [← h_eq] at h_take_p
  rw [h_take_u] at h_take_p
  exact h_take_p.symm

-- Therefore s = v as well
have h_s_eq_v : s = v := by
  have h_drop_s : (u ++ s).drop u.length = s := List.drop_left u s
  have h_drop_v : (p ++ v).drop p.length = v := List.drop_left p v
  rw [h_p_len] at h_drop_v
  rw [h_u_eq_p] at h_eq
  rw [← h_eq] at h_drop_v
  rw [h_drop_s] at h_drop_v
  exact h_drop_v.symm

-- Conclude w = u ++ v = u ⬝ v
rw [hs, h_s_eq_v]
```

```lean4
-- Extract the basic decompositions
obtain ⟨s, hs⟩ := (IsPrefix.def u w).mp hpre
obtain ⟨p, hp⟩ := (IsSuffix.def v w).mp hsuf

-- Establish that p.length = u.length using the length constraint
have h_p_len : p.length = u.length := by
  have h1 : w.length = u.length + s.length := by
    rw [hs]; exact List.length_append u s
  have h2 : w.length = p.length + v.length := by
    rw [hp]; exact List.length_append p v
  omega

-- We have u ++ s = w = p ++ v, so u ++ s = p ++ v
have h_eq : u ++ s = p ++ v := by
  rw [← hs, ← hp]

-- Since u.length = p.length, we can show u = p by extensionality
have h_u_eq_p : u = p := by
  apply List.ext_get
  · exact h_p_len.symm
  · intro i h1 h2
    have h_get : (u ++ s).get ⟨i, Nat.lt_add_right u.length h1⟩ = (p ++ v).get ⟨i, by rw [h_p_len]; exact Nat.lt_add_right p.length h2⟩ := by
      rw [h_eq]
    rw [List.get_append_left, List.get_append_left] at h_get
    exact h_get

-- Since u = p, we have u ++ s = u ++ v, so s = v
have h_s_eq_v : s = v := by
  have h_cancel : u ++ s = u ++ v := by
    rw [h_u_eq_p] at h_eq
    exact h_eq
  have h1 : s.length = v.length := by
    have h_len1 := congr_arg List.length h_cancel
    simp only [List.length_append] at h_len1
    omega
  apply List.ext_get
  · exact h1
  · intro i h_i1 h_i2
    have h_get : (u ++ s).get ⟨u.length + i, Nat.add_lt_add_left h_i1⟩ = (u ++ v).get ⟨u.length + i, by rw [h1] at h_i1; exact Nat.add_lt_add_left h_i1⟩ := by
      rw [h_cancel]
    rw [List.get_append_right, List.get_append_right] at h_get
    · simp only [Nat.add_sub_cancel] at h_get
      exact h_get
    · simp only [Nat.le_add_right]
    · simp only [Nat.le_add_right]

-- Conclude w = u ++ v = u ⬝ v
rw [hs, h_s_eq_v]
```

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that if `u` is a prefix of `v`, and `v` is a proper prefix of `w`, then `u` is a proper prefix of `w`. This is about the transitivity of prefix relations, but where the second relation is proper (strict).

From the CoW library:
1. `IsPrefix` means `u ≤ₚ v ↔ ∃ suffix, v = u ++ suffix`
2. `IsProperPrefix` means `u <ₚ w ↔ u ≤ₚ w ∧ u ≠ w`
3. There's also `IsProperPrefix.def` which gives `u <ₚ w ↔ ∃ v, w = u ++ v ∧ v ≠ []`

The goal is to show `u <ₚ w`, which means I need to prove both:
1. `u ≤ₚ w` (u is a prefix of w)
2. `u ≠ w` (u is not equal to w)

For part 1: I can use transitivity of the prefix relation. Since `u ≤ₚ v` and `v ≤ₚ w` (from the fact that `v <ₚ w` implies `v ≤ₚ w`), I get `u ≤ₚ w`.

For part 2: I need to show `u ≠ w`. I can use the fact that `v <ₚ w` means `v ≠ w`. If `u = w`, then since `u ≤ₚ v`, we'd have `w ≤ₚ v`. But combined with `v <ₚ w` (so `v ≤ₚ w`), this would give us `v = w`, contradicting `v <ₚ w`.

Let me think about this more carefully:
- From `huv : u ≤ₚ v`, get `v = u ++ s₁` for some `s₁`
- From `hvw : v <ₚ w`, get `w = v ++ s₂` for some `s₂ ≠ []`  
- So `w = (u ++ s₁) ++ s₂ = u ++ (s₁ ++ s₂)`
- Since `s₂ ≠ []`, we have `s₁ ++ s₂ ≠ []`, so `u <ₚ w`

This uses `IsProperPrefix.def` and basic list append properties.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this using the definitions of prefix and proper prefix, along with transitivity arguments.

**Step 1:** Show that `u ≤ₚ w` (regular prefix)
- From `hvw : v <ₚ w`, extract that `v ≤ₚ w` using `IsProperPrefix.isPrefix`
- Use transitivity: `IsPrefix.trans huv` with the prefix part of `hvw` to get `u ≤ₚ w`

**Step 2:** Show that `u ≠ w` 
- Use proof by contradiction: assume `u = w`
- From `huv : u ≤ₚ v` and the assumption `u = w`, we get `w ≤ₚ v`
- From `hvw : v <ₚ w`, we get `v ≤ₚ w`
- Combined with `w ≤ₚ v`, this would imply `v = w` (by antisymmetry of prefix on equal lengths or similar)
- But this contradicts `hvw : v <ₚ w`, which requires `v ≠ w`

- Since `s₂ ≠ []`, we have `s₁ ++ s₂ ≠ []`
- Therefore `u <ₚ w` by `IsProperPrefix.def`

**Step 3:** Combine the results
- Since we have both `u ≤ₚ w` and `u ≠ w`, we conclude `u <ₚ w`

**Key lemmas I'll reference:**
- `IsProperPrefix.isPrefix` (from CoW.ProperPrefix) - extracts the prefix part from proper prefix
- `IsPrefix.trans` (from CoW.Factor) - transitivity of prefix relation

--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that if `u` is a proper prefix of `v`, and `v` is a prefix of `w`, then `u` is a proper prefix of `w`. This is similar to the previous theorem but with the roles of proper/regular prefix swapped.

From the CoW library:
1. `IsProperPrefix` means `u <ₚ v ↔ u ≤ₚ v ∧ u ≠ v`
2. `IsProperPrefix.def` gives `u <ₚ v ↔ ∃ s, v = u ++ s ∧ s ≠ []`
3. `IsPrefix.def` gives `v ≤ₚ w ↔ ∃ t, w = v ++ t`

The goal is to show `u <ₚ w`, which means I need both:
1. `u ≤ₚ w` (u is a prefix of w)
2. `u ≠ w` (u is not equal to w)

My strategy:
- From `huv : u <ₚ v`, I get `v = u ++ s` where `s ≠ []`
- From `hvw : v ≤ₚ w`, I get `w = v ++ t` for some `t`
- Combining: `w = (u ++ s) ++ t = u ++ (s ++ t)`
- Since `s ≠ []`, we have `s ++ t ≠ []` (because the left part is non-empty)
- Therefore `u <ₚ w` by the definition

The key difference from the previous theorem is that here I need to show that if `s ≠ []`, then `s ++ t ≠ []` (where `s` is on the left). This should be even easier than the previous case where the non-empty part was on the right.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this using explicit decompositions, similar to the previous theorem but with the non-empty part appearing on the left of the concatenation.

**Step 1:** Extract decompositions from the hypotheses
- From `huv : u <ₚ v`, use `IsProperPrefix.def` to get `s` such that `v = u ++ s` and `s ≠ []`
- From `hvw : v ≤ₚ w`, use `IsPrefix.def` to get `t` such that `w = v ++ t`

**Step 2:** Construct the decomposition for `u <ₚ w`
- Apply `IsProperPrefix.def` in the reverse direction to show `u <ₚ w`
- Provide `s ++ t` as the witness suffix
- Need to prove: `w = u ++ (s ++ t)` and `s ++ t ≠ []`

**Step 3:** Prove the equality `w = u ++ (s ++ t)`
- Substitute the decompositions: `w = v ++ t = (u ++ s) ++ t`
- Use `List.append_assoc` to get `(u ++ s) ++ t = u ++ (s ++ t)`

**Step 4:** Prove that `s ++ t ≠ []`
- Since `s ≠ []`, use a Mathlib lemma like `List.append_ne_nil_of_left_ne_nil` (if it exists) or `List.ne_nil_of_append_ne_nil`
- This should state that if the left part of an append is non-empty, then the whole append is non-empty

**Alternative Step 4:** Direct reasoning
- Prove by contradiction: assume `s ++ t = []`
- By `List.append_eq_nil_iff` (or similar), this would require both `s = []` and `t = []`
- But we know `s ≠ []`, so we have a contradiction

**Key lemmas I'll reference:**
- `IsProperPrefix.def` (from CoW.ProperPrefix)
- `IsPrefix.def` (from CoW.Factor)
- `List.append_assoc` (from Mathlib)
- `List.append_ne_nil_of_left_ne_nil` or similar (from Mathlib)
- Potentially `List.append_eq_nil_iff` (from Mathlib)

**Note:** This should be slightly easier than the previous theorem since having the non-empty part on the left of the append makes the non-emptiness more direct to prove.

[18/45]   ProperPrefix | MEDIUM | proper_prefix_double_lt

[Orchestrator] Initiating Multi-Agent Repair for proper_prefix_double_lt
[Orchestrator] Loaded CoW source context (26169 chars).

==================================================
--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that if we have a chain of two proper prefix relations `u <ₚ v <ₚ w`, then `u.length + 2 ≤ w.length`. This means that each proper prefix step "costs" at least one character, so two steps cost at least two characters.

From the CoW library:
1. `IsProperPrefix.length_lt` gives us that if `a <ₚ b`, then `a.length < b.length`
2. `IsProperPrefix.def` gives us the explicit decomposition with non-empty witnesses

The key insight is that:
- From `u <ₚ v`, we get `u.length < v.length`, which means `v.length ≥ u.length + 1`
- From `v <ₚ w`, we get `v.length < w.length`, which means `w.length ≥ v.length + 1`
- Combining these: `w.length ≥ v.length + 1 ≥ (u.length + 1) + 1 = u.length + 2`

Alternatively, I could use the explicit decompositions:
- From `u <ₚ v`, get `v = u ++ s₁` where `s₁ ≠ []`, so `s₁.length ≥ 1`
- From `v <ₚ w`, get `w = v ++ s₂` where `s₂ ≠ []`, so `s₂.length ≥ 1`
- Then `w = u ++ s₁ ++ s₂`, so `w.length = u.length + s₁.length + s₂.length ≥ u.length + 1 + 1 = u.length + 2`

Both approaches should work, but the first using the length inequalities seems more direct.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this using the length inequalities provided by proper prefix relations and basic arithmetic.

**Step 1:** Extract the length inequalities from the proper prefix relations
- From `h1 : u <ₚ v`, use `IsProperPrefix.length_lt` to get `u.length < v.length`
- From `h2 : v <ₚ w`, use `IsProperPrefix.length_lt` to get `v.length < w.length`

**Step 2:** Convert the strict inequalities to non-strict bounds
- Since `u.length < v.length` in natural numbers, we have `u.length + 1 ≤ v.length`
- Since `v.length < w.length` in natural numbers, we have `v.length + 1 ≤ w.length`

**Step 3:** Chain the inequalities
- From step 2: `u.length + 1 ≤ v.length` and `v.length + 1 ≤ w.length`
- By transitivity: `u.length + 1 ≤ v.length` and `v.length + 1 ≤ w.length`
- Therefore: `u.length + 1 + 1 ≤ w.length`
- This simplifies to: `u.length + 2 ≤ w.length`

**Alternative approach using explicit decompositions:**

**Step 1:** Extract the proper prefix witnesses
- From `h1 : u <ₚ v`, use `IsProperPrefix.def` to get `s₁` with `v = u ++ s₁` and `s₁ ≠ []`
- From `h2 : v <ₚ w`, use `IsProperPrefix.def` to get `s₂` with `w = v ++ s₂` and `s₂ ≠ []`

**Step 2:** Combine the decompositions
- Substitute: `w = v ++ s₂ = (u ++ s₁) ++ s₂ = u ++ s₁ ++ s₂`
- Use `List.length_append` to get: `w.length = u.length + s₁.length + s₂.length`

**Step 3:** Use the fact that non-empty lists have positive length
- Since `s₁ ≠ []`, we have `s₁.length ≥ 1`
- Since `s₂ ≠ []`, we have `s₂.length ≥ 1`
- Therefore: `w.length = u.length + s₁.length + s₂.length ≥ u.length + 1 + 1 = u.length + 2`

**Key lemmas I'll reference:**
- `IsProperPrefix.length_lt` (from CoW.ProperPrefix)
- `Nat.succ_le_of_lt` (from Mathlib) - converts `a < b` to `a + 1 ≤ b` for naturals
- Basic arithmetic and transitivity of `≤`
- Alternatively: `IsProperPrefix.def`, `List.length_append`, and `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil`

**Note:** I'll use the first approach with length inequalities as it's more direct and doesn't require dealing with list decompositions.

```lean4
have h2_lt : v.length < w.length := h2.length_lt
have h1_le : u.length + 1 ≤ v.length := Nat.succ_le_of_lt h1_lt
have h2_le : v.length + 1 ≤ w.length := Nat.succ_le_of_lt h2_lt
calc u.length + 2 
    = (u.length + 1) + 1 := by ring
    _ ≤ v.length + 1     := Nat.add_le_add_right h1_le 1
    _ ≤ w.length         := h2_le
```

--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
I need to prove that if we have a chain of two proper suffix relations `u <ₛ v <ₛ w`, then `u.length + 2 ≤ w.length`. This is the suffix analogue of the proper prefix double length theorem.

From the CoW library:
1. `IsProperSuffix.length_lt` should give us that if `a <ₛ b`, then `a.length < b.length` (similar to the prefix case)
2. `IsProperSuffix.iff` gives us the explicit decomposition with non-empty witnesses

The key insight is the same as for the prefix case:
- From `u <ₛ v`, we get `u.length < v.length`, which means `v.length ≥ u.length + 1`
- From `v <ₛ w`, we get `v.length < w.length`, which means `w.length ≥ v.length + 1`
- Combining these: `w.length ≥ v.length + 1 ≥ (u.length + 1) + 1 = u.length + 2`

Alternatively, I could use the explicit decompositions:
- From `u <ₛ v`, get `v = p₁ ++ u` where `p₁ ≠ []`, so `p₁.length ≥ 1`
- From `v <ₛ w`, get `w = p₂ ++ v` where `p₂ ≠ []`, so `p₂.length ≥ 1`
- Then `w = p₂ ++ (p₁ ++ u) = (p₂ ++ p₁) ++ u`
- So `w.length = p₂.length + p₁.length + u.length ≥ 1 + 1 + u.length = u.length + 2`

Both approaches should work. I'll use the first approach with length inequalities since it's more direct and mirrors the prefix case exactly.

Actually, let me check if `IsProperSuffix.length_lt` exists. Looking at the CoW library, I see `IsProperPrefix.length_lt` but I need to make sure the suffix version exists or can be derived similarly.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
I will prove this using the length inequalities provided by proper suffix relations and basic arithmetic, mirroring the approach for the proper prefix double length theorem.

**Step 1:** Extract the length inequalities from the proper suffix relations
- From `h1 : u <ₛ v`, use `IsProperSuffix.length_lt` to get `u.length < v.length`
- From `h2 : v <ₛ w`, use `IsProperSuffix.length_lt` to get `v.length < w.length`

**Step 2:** Convert the strict inequalities to non-strict bounds
- Since `u.length < v.length` in natural numbers, we have `u.length + 1 ≤ v.length`
- Since `v.length < w.length` in natural numbers, we have `v.length + 1 ≤ w.length`

**Step 3:** Chain the inequalities
- From step 2: `u.length + 1 ≤ v.length` and `v.length + 1 ≤ w.length`
- By transitivity: `u.length + 1 ≤ v.length < w.length`
- This gives us: `u.length + 1 + 1 ≤ w.length`
- Therefore: `u.length + 2 ≤ w.length`

**Alternative approach using explicit decompositions (if length_lt is unavailable):**

**Step 1:** Extract the proper suffix witnesses
- From `h1 : u <ₛ v`, use `IsProperSuffix.iff` to get `p₁` with `v = p₁ ++ u` and `p₁ ≠ []`
- From `h2 : v <ₛ w`, use `IsProperSuffix.iff` to get `p₂` with `w = p₂ ++ v` and `p₂ ≠ []`

**Step 2:** Combine the decompositions
- Substitute: `w = p₂ ++ v = p₂ ++ (p₁ ++ u) = (p₂ ++ p₁) ++ u`
- Use `List.length_append` to get: `w.length = p₂.length + p₁.length + u.length`

**Step 3:** Use the fact that non-empty lists have positive length
- Since `p₁ ≠ []`, we have `p₁.length ≥ 1`
- Since `p₂ ≠ []`, we have `p₂.length ≥ 1`  
- Therefore: `w.length = p₂.length + p₁.length + u.length ≥ 1 + 1 + u.length = u.length + 2`

- `IsProperSuffix.length_lt` (from CoW.ProperSuffix, if available)

```lean4
have h2_lt : v.length < w.length := IsProperSuffix.length_lt h2
have h1_le : u.length + 1 ≤ v.length := Nat.succ_le_of_lt h1_lt
have h2_le : v.length + 1 ≤ w.length := Nat.succ_le_of_lt h2_lt
have : u.length + 1 < w.length := Nat.lt_of_le_of_lt h1_le h2_lt
omega
```

#### **Step 2: Apply logical negation rules**
Use classical logic to push the negation through the quantifier:
- `¬ (∀ b, P b)` is equivalent to `∃ b, ¬ P b`
- `¬ (P → Q)` is equivalent to `P ∧ ¬ Q`

which becomes `∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ ¬ (b = [])`
which is `∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ b ≠ []`

## Revised Proof Plan for `not_unbordered_iff_exists_nonempty_border`

### Problem Analysis
The logical transformation lemmas `not_forall` and `not_imp` are working, but there may be subtle definitional issues preventing automatic closure. We need a more robust approach.

### New Strategy: Manual Biconditional Proof

Since the logical structure is straightforward, let's prove both directions explicitly without relying on chained logical equivalences.

### Step-by-Step Plan

#### **Step 1: Set up biconditional proof**
- Use `constructor` to split into `→` and `←` directions

#### **Step 2: Forward direction (⟹)**
Prove: `¬ IsUnbordered w → ∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ b ≠ []`

```
intro h
-- h : ¬ IsUnbordered w
-- which unfolds to: ¬(∀ b, IsBorder b w → b = [])

-- Use classical reasoning: the negation of a universal implies an existential
by_contra hn
-- hn : ¬(∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ b ≠ [])

-- Push the negation inward
push_neg at hn
-- hn now becomes: ∀ b, IsBorder b w → b = []

-- But this means IsUnbordered w holds
-- Unfold IsUnbordered in h and apply hn
unfold IsUnbordered at h
exact h hn
```

#### **Step 3: Backward direction (⟸)**
Prove: `(∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ b ≠ []) → ¬ IsUnbordered w`

```
intro ⟨b, hb, hne⟩
-- b : Word α, hb : IsBorder b w, hne : b ≠ []

-- Unfold IsUnbordered to get the definition
unfold IsUnbordered

-- Show ¬(∀ b, IsBorder b w → b = [])
intro h_unb
-- h_unb : ∀ b, IsBorder b w → b = []

-- Apply h_unb to our specific b
have : b = [] := h_unb b hb

-- This contradicts hne
exact hne this
```

### Complete Proof Structure

```lean
constructor
· -- Forward: ¬ IsUnbordered w → ∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ b ≠ []
  intro h
  by_contra hn
  push_neg at hn
  unfold IsUnbordered at h
  exact h hn
  
· -- Backward: (∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ b ≠ []) → ¬ IsUnbordered w
  intro ⟨b, hb, hne⟩
  unfold IsUnbordered
  intro h_unb
  exact hne (h_unb b hb)
```

### Key Points
1. **No reliance on chained logical equivalences** - we prove each direction directly
2. **Explicit use of `by_contra` and `push_neg`** in forward direction - standard pattern for `¬∀ → ∃`
3. **Direct contradiction** in backward direction - straightforward
4. **Unfold `IsUnbordered` only when needed** - makes the goal clearer at each step

### Tactics Used
- `constructor` - split biconditional
- `intro` - introduce hypotheses
- `by_contra` - proof by contradiction
- `push_neg` - normalize negations (from Mathlib.Tactic.PushNeg)
- `unfold` - unfold definition of `IsUnbordered`
- `exact` - exact proof term

### No Special Lemmas Required
This approach uses only basic logical reasoning and the definition of `IsUnbordered` from the CoW library. No additional lemmas from the library are needed.

- `hsuf : u ≤ₛ w` (u is a suffix of w)
- `hlt : u.length < w.length` (u is strictly shorter than w)

1. `u ≤ₚ w` ✓ (we have this as `hpre`)
2. `u ≠ w` (we need to derive this)

Similarly for `u <ₛ w`:
1. `u ≤ₛ w` ✓ (we have this as `hsuf`)
2. `u ≠ w` (same inequality needed)

The length inequality `u.length < w.length` implies `u ≠ w` because if `u = w`, then `u.length = w.length`, contradicting the strict inequality.

**Proof strategy**:
1. First prove `u ≠ w` from the length inequality `hlt`
2. Construct `u <ₚ w` from `hpre` and `u ≠ w`
3. Construct `u <ₛ w` from `hsuf` and `u ≠ w`

   - For `u ≤ₚ w`: use `hpre` directly
   - For `u ≠ w`: prove by contradiction
     - Assume `u = w`
     - Then `u.length = w.length` (by substitution/rewrite)
     - This contradicts `hlt : u.length < w.length`
     - Conclude with `omega` or similar arithmetic tactic

3. **Prove `u <ₛ w`**:
   - By definition from `CoW/ProperSuffix.lean`, this is `u ≤ₛ w ∧ u ≠ w`
   - For `u ≤ₛ w`: use `hsuf` directly
   - For `u ≠ w`: same contradiction as in step 2

**Alternative cleaner approach**:
Since both proper prefix and proper suffix need the same `u ≠ w` proof, we could:
1. First establish `hne : u ≠ w` once (via `intro heq; subst heq; omega`)
2. Then construct `⟨⟨hpre, hne⟩, ⟨hsuf, hne⟩⟩` directly

**Referenced definitions**:
- `IsBorder` (definition from `CoW/Border.lean`)
- `IsProperPrefix` (definition from `CoW/ProperPrefix.lean`)  
- `IsProperSuffix` (definition from `CoW/ProperSuffix.lean`)

The implementation provided by the Builder is actually correct. If there's a verification issue, it may be environmental rather than logical.

Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that if `b` is a non-empty border of `w`, then `w.length ≥ 2`.

**Understanding what we have**:
1. `hb : IsBorder b w` means:
   - `b <ₚ w` (b is a proper prefix of w)
   - `b <ₛ w` (b is a proper suffix of w)

2. `hne : b ≠ []` means b has length at least 1

**Key observations**:

From `CoW/ProperPrefix.lean`, we have:
- `IsProperPrefix.length_lt : b <ₚ w → b.length < w.length`

From `CoW/ProperSuffix.lean`, we have:
- `IsProperSuffix.length_lt : b <ₛ w → b.length < w.length`

So from `hb`, we get `b.length < w.length`.

Also, since `b ≠ []`, we have `b.length ≥ 1` (or more precisely, `b.length > 0` or `1 ≤ b.length`).

From Mathlib, we know:
- `List.length_eq_zero` tells us `b.length = 0 ↔ b = []`
- So `b ≠ []` implies `b.length ≠ 0`, which means `b.length ≥ 1`

**Putting it together**:
- We have `b.length ≥ 1` (from `hne`)
- We have `b.length < w.length` (from `hb.1` using `IsProperPrefix.length_lt`)
- Therefore `1 < w.length`, which means `w.length ≥ 2`

Actually, more carefully:
- `b ≠ []` implies `0 < b.length` (using `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil`)
- Since `b.length` is a natural number and `0 < b.length`, we have `1 ≤ b.length`
- From `IsProperPrefix.length_lt`, we have `b.length < w.length`
- Combining: `1 ≤ b.length < w.length`
- Therefore: `1 < w.length`, i.e., `2 ≤ w.length`

   - This gives us: `b.length < w.length`

   - From `hne : b ≠ []`, we need to derive `1 ≤ b.length` (or equivalently `0 < b.length`)
   - Use `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` from Mathlib: this states `0 < length l ↔ l ≠ []`
   - Apply this to get `0 < b.length` from `hne`
   - This means `1 ≤ b.length`

4. **Combine the inequalities**:
   - We now have:
     - `1 ≤ b.length` (from step 3)
     - `b.length < w.length` (from step 2)
   - Chain these: `1 ≤ b.length < w.length`
   - This implies `1 < w.length`, which is equivalent to `2 ≤ w.length`
   - Close with `omega` or similar arithmetic tactic

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that if `b` is a border of `w`, then `w` has period `p = w.length - b.length`.

**Understanding the definitions**:

1. From `CoW/Border.lean`, `IsBorder b w` means:
   - `b <ₚ w`: b is a proper prefix of w
   - `b <ₛ w`: b is a proper suffix of w

2. From `CoW/ProperPrefix.lean`, `b <ₚ w` means:
   - There exists `v` such that `w = b ++ v` and `v ≠ []`
   - (via `IsProperPrefix.def`)

3. From `CoW/ProperSuffix.lean`, `b <ₛ w` means:
   - There exists `u` such that `w = u ++ b` and `u ≠ []`
   - (via `IsProperSuffix.iff`)

4. From `CoW/Period.lean`, `HasPeriod w p` means:
   - `0 < p` 
   - `∀ i : ℕ, i + p < w.length → w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)`

**Key insight - Border-Period correspondence**:

If `b` is a border of `w`, then:
- `w = b ++ v` for some non-empty `v` (from prefix property)
- `w = u ++ b` for some non-empty `u` (from suffix property)

The period `p = w.length - b.length` equals `v.length` (from the prefix decomposition) and also equals `u.length` (from the suffix decomposition).

From `w = b ++ v`, we have `w.length = b.length + v.length`, so `v.length = w.length - b.length = p`.

Similarly, from `w = u ++ b`, we have `w.length = u.length + b.length`, so `u.length = w.length - b.length = p`.

**Why does this give a period?**

The fact that `w = b ++ v = u ++ b` means:
- The first `b.length` positions of `w` are `b`
- The last `b.length` positions of `w` are also `b`
- Shifting by `p = w.length - b.length` positions maps the prefix overlap to the suffix overlap

For index `i`, if both `i` and `i + p` are valid indices (i.e., `i + p < w.length`):
- Position `i` is in the prefix part `b` (since `i < w.length - p = b.length`)
- Position `i + p` is in the suffix part starting at position `p = u.length`, which aligns with the suffix `b`
- Both access the same position in the border `b`, so `w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)`

**Proof strategy**:

1. Show `0 < p`:
   - From `IsProperPrefix.length_lt`, we have `b.length < w.length`
   - So `w.length - b.length > 0`

2. Show the periodicity condition:
   - Extract `v` from the proper prefix: `w = b ++ v` with `v ≠ []`
   - Extract `u` from the proper suffix: `w = u ++ b` with `u ≠ []`
   - Show that `v.length = u.length = p`
   - For any `i` with `i + p < w.length`, prove `w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)` using the overlap structure

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
**Goal**: Prove `HasPeriod w (w.length - b.length)` given `IsBorder b w`.

**Step-by-step strategy**:

1. **Define the period**: Let `p := w.length - b.length`

2. **Show `0 < p`** (first component of `HasPeriod`):
   - From `h : IsBorder b w`, extract `h.1 : b <ₚ w`
   - Apply `IsProperPrefix.length_lt` (from `CoW/ProperPrefix.lean`) to get `b.length < w.length`
   - Therefore `w.length - b.length > 0`, i.e., `0 < p`
   - Use `omega` or `Nat.sub_pos_of_lt`

3. **Extract the decompositions**:
   - From `h.1 : b <ₚ w`, use `IsProperPrefix.def` (from `CoW/ProperPrefix.lean`) to get:
     - `∃ v, w = b ++ v ∧ v ≠ []`
     - Let `v` be this witness
   - From `h.2 : b <ₛ w`, use `IsProperSuffix.iff` (from `CoW/ProperSuffix.lean`) to get:
     - `∃ u, w = u ++ b ∧ u ≠ []`
     - Let `u` be this witness

4. **Relate the decomposition lengths to p**:
   - From `w = b ++ v`, we have `w.length = b.length + v.length` (by `List.length_append`)
   - Therefore `v.length = w.length - b.length = p`
   - Similarly, from `w = u ++ b`, we have `u.length = w.length - b.length = p`

5. **Prove the periodicity condition** `∀ i, i + p < w.length → w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)`:
   - Consider any `i` with `i + p < w.length`
   - Since `p = w.length - b.length`, we have `i + (w.length - b.length) < w.length`
   - This simplifies to `i < b.length`
   - Using `w = b ++ v`, we have `w.get? i = (b ++ v).get? i = b.get? i` (since `i < b.length`)
   - Using `w = u ++ b` with `u.length = p`, we have:
     - `w.get? (i + p) = (u ++ b).get? (i + p)`
     - Since `i + p = i + u.length`, this accesses position `i` in the `b` part
     - So `w.get? (i + p) = b.get? i`
   - Therefore `w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)`

6. **Combine**: Construct `HasPeriod w p` from steps 2 and 5

**Referenced lemmas**:
- `IsBorder` (definition from `CoW/Border.lean`)
- `IsProperPrefix.def` (from `CoW/ProperPrefix.lean`)

- `IsProperSuffix.iff` (from `CoW/ProperSuffix.lean`)

- `List.get?_append` or `List.get?_append_left`/`List.get?_append_right` (from Mathlib for indexing)
- `HasPeriod` (definition from `CoW/Period.lean`)

**Key technical detail**: The indexing argument in step 5 requires careful use of `List.get?` lemmas for appended lists.

From Mathlib, the relevant lemmas are:
- `List.getElem?_append` or `List.get?_append`
- Potentially `List.get?_eq_get?` for rewriting

Actually, in Lean 4's List API:
- `List.get?_append` : `(l₁ ++ l₂).get? i = if i < l₁.length then l₁.get? i else l₂.get? (i - l₁.length)`
- Or there might be separate lemmas `List.get?_append_left` and `List.get?_append_right`

The issue is likely in the precise form of these lemmas. Let me revise the strategy to be more explicit about which Mathlib lemmas to use and how to apply them.

1. Use `List.get?_append` (the if-then-else version)
2. Simplify using the bounds we've established

- `(b ++ v).get? i = b.get? i` 
- `(u ++ b).get? (u.length + i) = b.get? i`

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
**Goal**: Prove `HasPeriod w (w.length - b.length)` given `IsBorder b w`.

**Revised step-by-step strategy** (with more precise Mathlib lemma references):

1. **Extract components and decompositions**:
   - From `h : IsBorder b w`, extract `h.1 : b <ₚ w` and `h.2 : b <ₛ w`
   - Apply `IsProperPrefix.def` to get `⟨v, hw_pre, hv_ne⟩` where `w = b ++ v` and `v ≠ []`
   - Apply `IsProperSuffix.iff` to get `⟨u, hw_suf, hu_ne⟩` where `w = u ++ b` and `u ≠ []`

2. **Define period and show `0 < p`**:
   - Set `p := w.length - b.length`
   - Use `IsProperPrefix.length_lt h.1` to get `b.length < w.length`
   - Therefore `0 < p` (by `omega` or `Nat.sub_pos`)

3. **Establish length relationships**:
   - From `w = b ++ v`: use `List.length_append` to get `w.length = b.length + v.length`
   - From `w = u ++ b`: use `List.length_append` to get `w.length = u.length + b.length`
   - Derive `u.length = p` and `v.length = p` (by `omega`)

4. **Prove periodicity using `List.get?_append`**:
   - For any `i` with `i + p < w.length`:
     - Show `i < b.length` (from `i + p < w.length` and `p = w.length - b.length`, use `omega`)
   
   - For the left side `w.get? i`:
     - Rewrite using `w = b ++ v`
     - Use `List.get?_append` from Mathlib: `(l₁ ++ l₂).get? i = if i < l₁.length then l₁.get? i else l₂.get? (i - l₁.length)`
     - Since `i < b.length`, the if-condition is true
     - Simplify to get `w.get? i = b.get? i`
   
   - For the right side `w.get? (i + p)`:
     - Rewrite using `w = u ++ b`
     - Since `u.length = p`, we have `i + p = i + u.length`
     - Use `List.get?_append` again
     - The condition `i + u.length < u.length` is false (since `i ≥ 0`)
     - So we get `(u ++ b).get? (i + u.length) = b.get? ((i + u.length) - u.length) = b.get? i`
   
   - Conclude `w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)`

5. **Construct the result**: Combine steps 2 and 4 to build `HasPeriod w p`

**Alternative simpler approach using `simp` and `split_ifs`**:
- After rewriting with `List.get?_append`, use `simp` with the established bounds
- Use `split_ifs` to handle the if-then-else in `List.get?_append`
- The bounds `i < b.length` and `¬(i + p < u.length)` should resolve the conditionals

- `IsProperPrefix.def` (from `CoW/ProperPrefix.lean`)

- `List.get?_append` (from Mathlib - the main indexing lemma)
- `HasPeriod` (definition from `CoW/Period.lean`)

**Key correction**: Use `List.get?_append` directly and handle the conditional with `split_ifs` or by simplification with the established bounds, rather than trying to use non-existent `_left`/`_right` variants.

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
The Builder is very close but struggling with the exact Mathlib lemma names and their application. Let me look at what's happening:

1. The Builder is trying to use `List.getElem?_append` which is the underlying implementation
2. But there's confusion between `List.get?` and `List.getElem?` - they might be related by a coercion

Let me think about the actual Mathlib lemmas available. In Lean 4's Mathlib:
- `List.get?` is notation/abbreviation for `List.getElem?`
- The key lemma should be something like `List.getElem?_append` or `List.get?_append`

But actually, looking at standard Mathlib, there might not be a single clean lemma. Instead, we might need to use:
- `List.getElem?_eq_getElem` when the index is in bounds
- Case analysis on whether `i < length l₁` for `(l₁ ++ l₂).get? i`

Actually, let me reconsider the approach entirely. Perhaps instead of wrestling with `get?` lemmas, we should:

1. Use the fact that `List.get?` returns `Option α`
2. Work with the explicit list structure
3. Or find a different characterization

Wait - I should check if there's a simpler approach using the **definition of period** more directly. Let me think about what `w.get? i` actually means for the concatenations:

For `w = b ++ v`:
- If `i < b.length`, then `w[i] = b[i]`
- If `i ≥ b.length`, then `w[i] = v[i - b.length]`

For `w = u ++ b`:
- If `i < u.length`, then `w[i] = u[i]`
- If `i ≥ u.length`, then `w[i] = b[i - u.length]`

The key insight: when `i < b.length` and `i + p < w.length` where `p = u.length`:
- `w[i]` from the prefix decomposition gives `b[i]`
- `w[i + u.length]` from the suffix decomposition: since `i + u.length ≥ u.length`, this gives `b[(i + u.length) - u.length] = b[i]`

Maybe I should suggest using `List.getElem_append` (with `get` instead of `get?`) after proving the indices are in bounds, or use a completely different set of lemmas.

Let me provide a cleaner strategy that avoids the `get?_append` complexity.

**Goal**: Prove `HasPeriod w (w.length - b.length)` given `IsBorder b w`.

**Revised strategy using a different approach**:

Since the Builder is struggling with `List.get?_append`, let me suggest an alternative that works more directly with the list structure.

**Main insight**: Instead of using `get?_append`, we can use the fact that for lists, `get?` can be computed by cases on the list structure, or we can work with explicit `get` (with proof of bounds) and convert.

**Step-by-step**:

1. **Extract components** (same as before):
   - From `h : IsBorder b w`, get decompositions:
     - `w = b ++ v` with `v ≠ []` (from proper prefix)
     - `w = u ++ b` with `u ≠ []` (from proper suffix)
   - Set `p := w.length - b.length`

2. **Show `0 < p`**:
   - Use `IsProperPrefix.length_lt h.1` to get `b.length < w.length`
   - Therefore `0 < p` by `omega`

3. **Establish `u.length = p`**:
   - From both decompositions, derive length equations
   - Show `u.length = w.length - b.length = p`

4. **For the periodicity condition**, use **case analysis on Option**:
   - For any `i` with `i + p < w.length`:
   - Show `i < b.length` (from the inequality)
   
   **Key observation**: Both `w.get? i` and `w.get? (i+p)` should return `some (b.get i ...)` since both indices access the border `b`.
   
   - Use `List.get?_eq_some` from Mathlib, which states:
     `l.get? n = some a ↔ ∃ h : n < l.length, l.get ⟨n, h⟩ = a`
   
   - For `w.get? i` with `w = b ++ v`:
     - Since `i < b.length < w.length`, we can use `List.get_append_left`:
       `(l ++ l').get ⟨i, h⟩ = l.get ⟨i, h'⟩` when `i < l.length`
   
   - For `w.get? (i+p)` with `w = u ++ b` and `p = u.length`:
     - We have `i + p = i + u.length`
     - Since `i + u.length ≥ u.length`, use `List.get_append_right`:
       `(l ++ l').get ⟨i, h⟩ = l'.get ⟨i - l.length, h'⟩` when `i ≥ l.length`
     - This gives `b.get ⟨(i + u.length) - u.length, _⟩ = b.get ⟨i, _⟩`

5. **Conclude**: Both equal the same element of `b`, so they're equal as `Option α`

**Alternative even simpler approach**: 

Instead of fighting with `get?`, directly prove using the **infix decomposition**:

Since `w = b ++ v = u ++ b` with `u.length = p`:
- The first `b.length` positions of `w` are exactly `b`
- Starting from position `p = u.length`, the next `b.length` positions are also `b`
- Therefore positions `i` and `i + p` (when both valid and `i < b.length`) access the same position in `b`

This can be formalized using:
- `List.get?_of_eq` : convert equality of lists to equality of `get?`
- Or `List.ext_get?` to show list equality

**Referenced Mathlib lemmas to try**:
- `List.get_append_left` : `(l ++ l').get ⟨i, h⟩ = l.get ⟨i, h'⟩` (when `i < l.length`)
- `List.get_append_right` : accessing beyond first list
- `List.get?_eq_some` : relationship between `get?` and `get`
- `List.get?_len_le` : when index is too large, `get?` returns `none`

**Simplest tactical approach**:
Use `cases` on the `get?` results and prove both are `some` with the same value, or use `ext` lemmas if available.

**Goal**: Prove that if `b` is a border of `w` and `b ≠ []`, then `w` is not unbordered.

**Definitions to recall**:
1. `IsBorder b w` means `b <ₚ w ∧ b <ₛ w` (both proper prefix and proper suffix)
2. `IsUnbordered w` means `∀ b, IsBorder b w → b = []`

**Logical structure**:
- We need to prove `¬ IsUnbordered w`
- By definition, this means `¬ (∀ b, IsBorder b w → b = [])`
- Equivalently, `∃ b, IsBorder b w ∧ b ≠ []`
- We already have such a `b` from our hypotheses: `h : IsBorder b w` and `hne : b ≠ []`

**Proof strategy**:
1. Unfold `IsUnbordered w` to get `∀ b, IsBorder b w → b = []`
2. Apply this universal statement to our specific border `b`
3. We get `b = []`
4. But we also have `hne : b ≠ []`
5. This is a contradiction

This is a straightforward proof by contradiction using the definition of `IsUnbordered`.

**Lemmas I can use**:
- The definition of `IsUnbordered` from the context
- Basic logical reasoning (`intro`, `apply`, contradiction)
- No special CoW lemmas needed beyond the definitions

[Orchestrator] Loaded CoW source context (26169 chars).

==================================================
--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that if `u` is conjugate to the empty word, then `u` is empty.

**Understanding conjugacy**:

From `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`:
```lean
def IsConjugate (u v : Word α) : Prop :=
  ∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ v = y ⬝ x
```

So `u ~ []` means: `∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ [] = y ⬝ x`

**Key observation**:

If `[] = y ⬝ x`, then since `[]` is the empty word and concatenation gives us:
- `[].length = 0`
- `(y ⬝ x).length = y.length + x.length` (by `Word.length_concat`)

Therefore: `0 = y.length + x.length`

Since lengths are natural numbers, this means both `y.length = 0` and `x.length = 0`.

By `List.length_eq_zero`, this gives us `y = []` and `x = []`.

Then `u = x ⬝ y = [] ⬝ [] = []`.

**Proof strategy**:

1. Unfold the conjugacy definition to get witnesses `x` and `y`
2. From `[] = y ⬝ x`, derive length equation `0 = y.length + x.length`
3. Conclude `y = []` and `x = []`
4. Substitute into `u = x ⬝ y` to get `u = []`

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
**Goal**: Prove `u = []` given `h : u ~ []`.

**Step-by-step strategy**:

1. **Unfold conjugacy definition**:
   - From `h : u ~ []`, use the definition `IsConjugate` (from `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`)
   - This gives: `∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ [] = y ⬝ x`
   - Use `obtain ⟨x, y, hu, hempty⟩ := h` to extract witnesses

2. **Analyze the empty word equation**:
   - We have `hempty : [] = y ⬝ x`
   - Take length of both sides: `congr_arg Word.length hempty`
   - This gives: `[].length = (y ⬝ x).length`
   - Simplify using:
     - `Word.length_empty : ([] : Word α).length = 0`
     - `Word.length_concat : (y ⬝ x).length = y.length + x.length`
   - Get: `0 = y.length + x.length`

3. **Derive that both words are empty**:
   - From `0 = y.length + x.length` and properties of natural numbers:
     - Both `y.length = 0` and `x.length = 0` (by `omega` or `Nat.eq_zero_of_add_eq_zero`)
   - Apply `List.length_eq_zero` (from Mathlib):
     - `y.length = 0` implies `y = []`
     - `x.length = 0` implies `x = []`

4. **Conclude**:
   - Substitute `x = []` and `y = []` into `hu : u = x ⬝ y`
   - Get `u = [] ⬝ []`
   - Simplify using `Word.concat_empty_left` or `Word.concat_empty_right`
   - Conclude `u = []`

**Referenced lemmas**:
- `IsConjugate` (definition from `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`)
- `Word.length_empty` (from `CoW/Word.lean`)
- `Word.length_concat` (from `CoW/Word.lean`)
- `Word.concat_empty_left` and/or `Word.concat_empty_right` (from `CoW/Word.lean`)
- `List.length_eq_zero` (from Mathlib) - states `l.length = 0 ↔ l = []`
- Arithmetic via `omega` or `Nat.eq_zero_of_add_eq_zero_left/right`

**Alternative simpler path**:
- Since `[] = y ⬝ x` and concatenation of non-empty words is non-empty, both must be empty
- Can use `List.append_eq_nil` from Mathlib: `l ++ l' = [] ↔ l = [] ∧ l' = []`
- Apply to `hempty : [] = y ++ x` (after converting `⬝` notation)
- Get `y = []` and `x = []` directly
- Then substitute into `u = x ⬝ y`

This alternative is cleaner and uses a direct Mathlib lemma.

```lean4
have hyx : y = [] ∧ x = [] := List.append_eq_nil.mp hempty.symm
```

[31/45]      Conjugacy | MEDIUM | conjugate_concat_rotate

[Orchestrator] Initiating Multi-Agent Repair for conjugate_concat_rotate
[Orchestrator] Loaded CoW source context (26169 chars).

==================================================
--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that `(u ⬝ v ⬝ w) ~ (v ⬝ w ⬝ u)`, i.e., these words are conjugate.

**Understanding conjugacy**:

From `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`:
```lean
def IsConjugate (u v : Word α) : Prop :=
  ∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ v = y ⬝ x
```

So we need to find words `x` and `y` such that:
- `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = x ⬝ y`
- `v ⬝ w ⬝ u = y ⬝ x`

**Key insight**:

This is a cyclic rotation. If we choose:
- `x = u ⬝ v ⬝ w` 
- `y = []` (empty word)

Wait, that doesn't work because then `v ⬝ w ⬝ u ≠ [] ⬝ (u ⬝ v ⬝ w)`.

Let me think differently. We want to show that `(u ⬝ v ⬝ w)` and `(v ⬝ w ⬝ u)` are conjugate.

Actually, the pattern is clearer if we think of it as:
- First word: `u ⬝ (v ⬝ w)`
- Second word: `(v ⬝ w) ⬝ u`

So with `x = u` and `y = v ⬝ w`, we have:
- `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w) = x ⬝ y`
- `v ⬝ w ⬝ u = (v ⬝ w) ⬝ u = y ⬝ x`

This works! We just need to use associativity of concatenation to rearrange the parentheses.

**Proof strategy**:

1. Choose witnesses: `x := u` and `y := v ⬝ w`
2. Show `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = x ⬝ y`:
   - `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w)` by associativity
   - `= x ⬝ y` by substitution
3. Show `v ⬝ w ⬝ u = y ⬝ x`:
   - `v ⬝ w ⬝ u = (v ⬝ w) ⬝ u` by associativity
   - `= y ⬝ x` by substitution
4. Combine to construct the conjugacy

Actually, looking at the expressions:
- `u ⬝ v ⬝ w` is parsed as `(u ⬝ v) ⬝ w` (left-associative)
- `v ⬝ w ⬝ u` is parsed as `(v ⬝ w) ⬝ u` (left-associative)

So we need to rearrange to get the right form. Let me reconsider:

For `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = (u ⬝ v) ⬝ w`, we want to write it as `x ⬝ y` where:
- `x = u ⬝ v`
- `y = w`

Hmm, but then `y ⬝ x = w ⬝ (u ⬝ v) = w ⬝ u ⬝ v ≠ v ⬝ w ⬝ u`.

Let me try again with:
- `x = u`
- `y = v ⬝ w`

Then:
- `x ⬝ y = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w)` - need to show this equals `(u ⬝ v) ⬝ w`
  - By associativity: `u ⬝ (v ⬝ w) = (u ⬝ v) ⬝ w` ✓
- `y ⬝ x = (v ⬝ w) ⬝ u` - need to show this equals `(v ⬝ w) ⬝ u`
  - This is already in the right form ✓

Perfect! So the witnesses are `x = u` and `y = v ⬝ w`.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
**Goal**: Prove `(u ⬝ v ⬝ w) ~ (v ⬝ w ⬝ u)`.

**Step-by-step strategy**:

1. **Unfold conjugacy definition**:
   - We need to exhibit witnesses `x` and `y` such that:
     - `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = x ⬝ y`
     - `v ⬝ w ⬝ u = y ⬝ x`

2. **Choose witnesses**:
   - Let `x := u`
   - Let `y := v ⬝ w`

3. **Prove first equation** `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = x ⬝ y`:
   - LHS: `u ⬝ v ⬝ w` is parsed as `(u ⬝ v) ⬝ w` (left-associative)
   - RHS: `x ⬝ y = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w)`
   - Use `Word.concat_assoc` (from `CoW/Word.lean`): `(u ⬝ v) ⬝ w = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w)`
   - Therefore `u ⬝ v ⬝ w = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w) = x ⬝ y`

4. **Prove second equation** `v ⬝ w ⬝ u = y ⬝ x`:
   - LHS: `v ⬝ w ⬝ u` is parsed as `(v ⬝ w) ⬝ u`
   - RHS: `y ⬝ x = (v ⬝ w) ⬝ u`
   - These are definitionally equal (by substitution of `y` and `x`)
   - Or use `rfl` after substitution

5. **Construct the conjugacy**:
   - Apply the definition of `IsConjugate`
   - Provide witnesses: `⟨u, v ⬝ w, eq1, eq2⟩` where `eq1` and `eq2` are the equations from steps 3 and 4

**Referenced lemmas**:
- `IsConjugate` (definition from `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`)
- `Word.concat_assoc` (from `CoW/Word.lean`): `(u ⬝ v) ⬝ w = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w)`

```lean

constructor
· -- Show (u ⬝ v) ⬝ w = u ⬝ (v ⬝ w)
  exact (Word.concat_assoc u v w).symm
· -- Show (v ⬝ w) ⬝ u = (v ⬝ w) ⬝ u
  rfl
```

Or more compactly:


--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that if `u` is conjugate to `v` and `u ≠ []`, then `v ≠ []`.

**Understanding conjugacy**:

From `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`:
```lean
def IsConjugate (u v : Word α) : Prop :=
  ∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ v = y ⬝ x
```

So `u ~ v` means: `∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ v = y ⬝ x`

**Key observation**:

From `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`, we have:
```lean
lemma IsConjugate.length_eq {u v : Word α} (h : u ~ v) : u.length = v.length
```

This is perfect! If conjugate words have equal length, and `u ≠ []` means `u.length > 0`, then `v.length > 0`, which means `v ≠ []`.

**Proof strategy**:

1. Use `IsConjugate.length_eq` to get `u.length = v.length`
2. From `u ≠ []`, derive `u.length > 0` (or `0 < u.length`)
3. By equality, `v.length > 0`
4. Therefore `v ≠ []`

**Details**:

- To get `u.length > 0` from `u ≠ []`, use `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` from Mathlib
- To get `v ≠ []` from `v.length > 0`, use the same lemma in reverse

Actually, even simpler: we can prove by contradiction:
- Assume `v = []`
- Then `v.length = 0`
- By `IsConjugate.length_eq`, `u.length = 0`
- So `u = []` (by `List.length_eq_zero`)
- Contradiction with `hu : u ≠ []`

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
**Goal**: Prove `v ≠ []` given `h : u ~ v` and `hu : u ≠ []`.

**Strategy**: Use the fact that conjugate words have equal length.

**Step-by-step**:

1. **Apply length equality lemma**:
   - Use `IsConjugate.length_eq` (from `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`) on `h : u ~ v`
   - Get `hlen : u.length = v.length`

2. **Proof by contradiction**:
   - Assume `hv : v = []` (for contradiction)
   - Then `v.length = 0` (by `Word.length_empty` or direct simplification)
   - By `hlen`, we have `u.length = 0`
   - Apply `List.length_eq_zero` (from Mathlib): `u.length = 0 → u = []`
   - Get `u = []`
   - Contradiction with `hu : u ≠ []`

**Alternative direct approach** (without contradiction):

1. **Get length equality**: `hlen : u.length = v.length` (from `IsConjugate.length_eq`)

2. **Derive positive length of u**:
   - From `hu : u ≠ []`, use `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` (from Mathlib)
   - Get `0 < u.length`

3. **Transfer to v**:
   - By `hlen`, we have `0 < v.length`
   - By `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` in reverse, get `v ≠ []`

**Referenced lemmas**:
- `IsConjugate.length_eq` (from `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`) - **key lemma**
- `Word.length_empty` or `List.length_eq_zero` (from Mathlib) - for `[].length = 0` and `length = 0 → list = []`
- `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil` (from Mathlib) - for `0 < length ↔ list ≠ []`

**Recommended implementation** (proof by contradiction is cleaner):
```lean
intro hv
have hlen := IsConjugate.length_eq h
rw [hv] at hlen
simp at hlen
have : u = [] := List.length_eq_zero.mp hlen
exact hu this
```

Or more concisely:
```lean
intro hv
have := IsConjugate.length_eq h
simp [hv] at this
exact hu (List.length_eq_zero.mp this)
```

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that `w ^ (n + 1) ~ w ⬝ w ^ n`, i.e., these words are conjugate.

**Understanding the power operation**:

From `CoW/Word.lean`:
```lean
lemma Word.pow_succ (w : Word α) (n : ℕ) : w ^ (n + 1) = w ^ n ⬝ w
```

So `w ^ (n + 1) = w ^ n ⬝ w` (right concatenation, following Mathlib's convention).

**What we need to show**:

We need to prove `(w ^ n ⬝ w) ~ (w ⬝ w ^ n)`.

This is exactly a conjugacy: we're showing that concatenating `w` on the right vs. on the left gives conjugate words.

**Understanding conjugacy**:

From `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`:
```lean
def IsConjugate (u v : Word α) : Prop :=
  ∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ v = y ⬝ x
```

**Strategy**:

For `(w ^ n ⬝ w) ~ (w ⬝ w ^ n)`, we need witnesses `x` and `y` such that:
- `w ^ n ⬝ w = x ⬝ y`
- `w ⬝ w ^ n = y ⬝ x`

The natural choice is:
- `x = w ^ n`
- `y = w`

Then:
- `x ⬝ y = w ^ n ⬝ w` ✓ (matches LHS)
- `y ⬝ x = w ⬝ w ^ n` ✓ (matches RHS)

So we just need to exhibit these witnesses!

**Detailed proof strategy**:

1. Rewrite `w ^ (n + 1)` using `Word.pow_succ`: `w ^ (n + 1) = w ^ n ⬝ w`
2. Now we need to show `(w ^ n ⬝ w) ~ (w ⬝ w ^ n)`
3. Apply the definition of `IsConjugate` with witnesses `x = w ^ n` and `y = w`
4. The two equations are definitional equalities (or use `rfl`)

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that if `u ~ v`, then `u ^ n ~ v ^ n` for any natural number `n`.

**Understanding conjugacy**:

From `CoW/Conjugacy.lean`:
```lean
def IsConjugate (u v : Word α) : Prop :=
  ∃ x y : Word α, u = x ⬝ y ∧ v = y ⬝ x
```

Also from the library:
```lean
lemma IsConjugate.refl (w : Word α) : w ~ w
lemma IsConjugate.symm {u v : Word α} (h : u ~ v) : v ~ u
```

There's a note that transitivity is marked as FUTURE WORK, so we can't rely on that.

**Key observation**:

If `u = x ⬝ y` and `v = y ⬝ x`, then:
- `u ^ n = (x ⬝ y) ^ n`
- `v ^ n = (y ⬝ x) ^ n`

We need to show these are conjugate.

**Strategy - Induction on n**:

This is a natural candidate for induction on `n`.

**Base case** (`n = 0`):
- `u ^ 0 = []` (by `Word.pow_zero`)
- `v ^ 0 = []` (by `Word.pow_zero`)
- `[] ~ []` (by `IsConjugate.refl`)

**Inductive step** (`n → n + 1`):
- IH: `u ^ n ~ v ^ n`
- Want to show: `u ^ (n + 1) ~ v ^ (n + 1)`
- By `Word.pow_succ`: `u ^ (n + 1) = u ^ n ⬝ u` and `v ^ (n + 1) = v ^ n ⬝ v`
- We have `u ~ v` (given `h`)
- We have `u ^ n ~ v ^ n` (IH)
- Need to show: `(u ^ n ⬝ u) ~ (v ^ n ⬝ v)`

The issue is: we need to combine two conjugacy relations, which would typically require transitivity. But transitivity is marked as FUTURE WORK in the library!

**Alternative approach - Direct construction**:

From `h : u ~ v`, we have witnesses `x, y` such that `u = x ⬝ y` and `v = y ⬝ x`.

We want to show `u ^ n ~ v ^ n`.

Let me think about what `(x ⬝ y) ^ n` and `(y ⬝ x) ^ n` look like...

For small values:
- `(x ⬝ y) ^ 1 = x ⬝ y` and `(y ⬝ x) ^ 1 = y ⬝ x` - these are conjugate by definition ✓
- `(x ⬝ y) ^ 2 = (x ⬝ y) ⬝ (x ⬝ y) = x ⬝ y ⬝ x ⬝ y`
- `(y ⬝ x) ^ 2 = (y ⬝ x) ⬝ (y ⬝ x) = y ⬝ x ⬝ y ⬝ x`

For `(x ⬝ y) ^ 2 ~ (y ⬝ x) ^ 2`:
- We'd need `x ⬝ y ⬝ x ⬝ y = a ⬝ b` and `y ⬝ x ⬝ y ⬝ x = b ⬝ a`
- Try `a = x`, `b = y ⬝ x ⬝ y`... let's check
- No wait, this is getting complicated.

**Better approach - use morphism/power preservation**:

Actually, looking at the structure, maybe we can prove this by showing that conjugacy is preserved under concatenation (though we'd need that lemma), or by a more direct inductive argument.

**Practical induction without transitivity**:

Let me try induction but be more careful:

For `u ^ (n+1) ~ v ^ (n+1)`:
- `u ^ (n+1) = u ^ n ⬝ u`
- `v ^ (n+1) = v ^ n ⬝ v`

From `h : u ~ v`, we have `u = x ⬝ y`, `v = y ⬝ x`.
From IH: `u ^ n ~ v ^ n`, we have `u ^ n = a ⬝ b`, `v ^ n = b ⬝ a` for some `a, b`.

Then:
- `u ^ (n+1) = (a ⬝ b) ⬝ (x ⬝ y)`
- `v ^ (n+1) = (b ⬝ a) ⬝ (y ⬝ x)`

Can we show these are conjugate directly? This requires careful manipulation with associativity...

Actually, this might be quite involved. Let me suggest a plan that acknowledges the complexity.

Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that `w ⬝ w` is not primitive (for non-empty `w`).

**Understanding primitivity**:

From `CoW/Period.lean`:
```lean
def IsPrimitive (w : Word α) : Prop :=
  w ≠ [] ∧ ¬ ∃ (u : Word α) (k : ℕ), k ≥ 2 ∧ w = u ^ k
```

So a word is primitive if:
1. It's non-empty
2. It's not a proper power (i.e., can't be written as `u^k` with `k ≥ 2`)

To show `w ⬝ w` is NOT primitive, we need to show the negation:
- Either `w ⬝ w = []` (but this is false when `w ≠ []`)
- OR `w ⬝ w` IS a proper power

**Key observation**:

`w ⬝ w = w ^ 2` by the power notation!

Actually, let me check: from `CoW/Word.lean`, we have the Monoid instance where `*` is concatenation. And powers are defined via the Monoid structure.

So `w ^ 2 = w ^ (1 + 1) = w ^ 1 * w = w * w = w ⬝ w` (using `Word.pow_succ` and `Word.pow_one`).

**Therefore**: `w ⬝ w = w ^ 2`, which is a proper power with exponent `k = 2 ≥ 2`.

**Proof strategy**:

1. Show that `w ⬝ w` is a proper power by exhibiting `u = w` and `k = 2`
2. This directly contradicts `IsPrimitive (w ⬝ w)`

**Steps**:

1. Unfold `IsPrimitive (w ⬝ w)`: this means `w ⬝ w ≠ [] ∧ ¬∃ u k, k ≥ 2 ∧ w ⬝ w = u ^ k`
2. To prove the negation, we show `∃ u k, k ≥ 2 ∧ w ⬝ w = u ^ k`
3. Choose `u = w` and `k = 2`
4. Need to verify:
   - `k = 2 ≥ 2` ✓ (trivial)
   - `w ⬝ w = w ^ 2` (need to prove this)

For the last part, we can use:
- `w ^ 2 = w ^ (1 + 1)` (definitional)
- `= w ^ 1 * w` (by `Word.pow_succ` or `pow_succ`)
- `= w * w` (by `Word.pow_one` or `pow_one`)
- `= w ⬝ w` (since `*` is `⬝` in the Monoid instance)

**Goal**: Prove `¬ IsPrimitive (w ⬝ w)` given `hw : w ≠ []`.

**Key insight**: `w ⬝ w = w ^ 2`, which is a proper power (exponent = 2), so it can't be primitive.

**Step-by-step strategy**:

1. **Unfold the definition of `IsPrimitive`**:
   - From `CoW/Period.lean`: `IsPrimitive v` means `v ≠ [] ∧ ¬∃ u k, k ≥ 2 ∧ v = u ^ k`
   - We want to prove `¬ IsPrimitive (w ⬝ w)`
   - This is equivalent to: `w ⬝ w = [] ∨ (∃ u k, k ≥ 2 ∧ w ⬝ w = u ^ k)`

2. **Use `IsPrimitive.not_proper_power`**:
   - From the library: `IsPrimitive.not_proper_power {w} (h : IsPrimitive w) : ¬∃ (u : Word α) (k : ℕ), k ≥ 2 ∧ w = u ^ k`
   - Contrapositive: if `∃ u k, k ≥ 2 ∧ w = u ^ k`, then `¬ IsPrimitive w`

     - `= w ^ 1 * w` (by `pow_succ w 1`)

   - `intro h_prim` (assume `IsPrimitive (w ⬝ w)`)
   - Apply `IsPrimitive.not_proper_power h_prim`
   - Get contradiction with the witness `⟨w, 2, ⟨le_refl 2, proof_of_eq⟩⟩`

--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that `w ⬝ w` has period `w.length` when `w ≠ []`.

**Understanding period**:

From `CoW/Period.lean`:
```lean
def HasPeriod (w : Word α) (p : ℕ) : Prop :=
  0 < p ∧ ∀ i : ℕ, i + p < w.length → w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)
```

So `HasPeriod (w ⬝ w) w.length` means:
1. `0 < w.length` (w is non-empty)
2. For all `i`, if `i + w.length < (w ⬝ w).length`, then `(w ⬝ w).get? i = (w ⬝ w).get? (i + w.length)`

**Key observations**:

1. `(w ⬝ w).length = w.length + w.length = 2 * w.length` (by `Word.length_concat`)

2. The condition `i + w.length < 2 * w.length` simplifies to `i < w.length`

3. For `i < w.length`:
   - `(w ⬝ w).get? i` accesses position `i` in the first copy of `w`, so `(w ⬝ w).get? i = w.get? i`
   - `(w ⬝ w).get? (i + w.length)` accesses position `i + w.length` in `w ⬝ w`, which is position `i` in the second copy of `w`, so `(w ⬝ w).get? (i + w.length) = w.get? i`
   - Therefore they're equal!

**Proof strategy**:

1. Show `0 < w.length`:
   - From `hw : w ≠ []`, use `List.length_pos_iff_ne_nil`

2. Show the periodicity condition:
   - Take any `i` with `i + w.length < (w ⬝ w).length`
   - Use `Word.length_concat` to rewrite `(w ⬝ w).length = 2 * w.length`
   - From `i + w.length < 2 * w.length`, derive `i < w.length`
   - Show `(w ⬝ w).get? i = w.get? i` using `List.get?_append` (left part)
   - Show `(w ⬝ w).get? (i + w.length) = w.get? i` using `List.get?_append` (right part)

**Details for the `get?` computations**:

For `(w ⬝ w).get? i` where `i < w.length`:
- Since `w ⬝ w = w ++ w`, use `List.get?_append`
- The condition `i < w.length` means we access the left part
- So `(w ++ w).get? i = w.get? i`

For `(w ⬝ w).get? (i + w.length)`:
- Since `i + w.length ≥ w.length`, we access the right part
- Use `List.get?_append` for the case when index ≥ first list length
- `(w ++ w).get? (i + w.length) = w.get? ((i + w.length) - w.length) = w.get? i`

--- Planning Phase (Iteration 1/3) ---

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Let me analyze this theorem step-by-step.

**Goal**: Prove that if `w` has period `p`, then `w` also has period `k * p` for any `k ≥ 1`.

**Understanding period**:

From `CoW/Period.lean`:
```lean
def HasPeriod (w : Word α) (p : ℕ) : Prop :=
  0 < p ∧ ∀ i : ℕ, i + p < w.length → w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)
```

**What we have**:
- `hp : HasPeriod w p`, which means:
  - `0 < p`
  - `∀ i, i + p < w.length → w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)`
- `hk : 1 ≤ k`

**What we need to show**:
- `HasPeriod w (k * p)`, which means:
  - `0 < k * p`
  - `∀ i, i + k * p < w.length → w.get? i = w.get? (i + k * p)`

**Key insight**:

If `w` has period `p`, then:
- `w[i] = w[i + p]` for valid indices
- By repeatedly applying this, `w[i] = w[i + p] = w[i + 2p] = ... = w[i + kp]`

More formally, for any `i` where `i + k * p < w.length`:
- We can show `w[i] = w[i + k * p]` by repeatedly using the period `p` property `k` times

**Proof strategy**:

1. **Show `0 < k * p`**:
   - From `hk : 1 ≤ k` and `hp.1 : 0 < p`
   - We have `k * p ≥ 1 * p = p > 0`
   - Use `Nat.mul_pos` or similar

2. **Show the periodicity condition**:
   - Take any `i` with `i + k * p < w.length`
   - We want to prove `w.get? i = w.get? (i + k * p)`
   
   **Strategy**: Induction on `k` (or use iteration)
   
   - Base case `k = 1`: 
     - `i + 1 * p = i + p`
     - Directly from `hp.2`
   
   - Inductive step `k → k + 1`:
     - Want to show `w.get? i = w.get? (i + (k + 1) * p)`
     - Note `(k + 1) * p = k * p + p`
     - By IH: `w.get? i = w.get? (i + k * p)` (if `i + k * p < w.length`)
     - By period property: `w.get? (i + k * p) = w.get? (i + k * p + p)` (if valid)
     - Transitivity gives the result
   
   Wait, but we don't have transitivity of equality built in... actually, we do! Equality is always transitive.

**Alternative simpler approach**:

Actually, we can prove this more directly without explicit induction by using the fact that we can "chain" the period property:

For `i + k * p < w.length`:
- If `k = 1`: use `hp` directly
- If `k > 1`: repeatedly apply the period property

But the cleanest is probably induction on `k`.

**Detailed induction plan**:

Since we have `hk : 1 ≤ k`, we can do induction starting from 1, or we can use strong induction.

Actually, simpler: since `k ≥ 1`, we can write `k = n + 1` for some `n ≥ 0`, then do induction on `n`.

Or even simpler: just do direct induction on `k` and handle `k = 0` as a trivial case (though we're given `k ≥ 1`).

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
No specific plan block detected.


--- Builder Execution (Attempt 1/3) ---
[Orchestrator] ⚠️  Proof contains 'sorry' — treating as incomplete.
[BuilderAgent] 🛠️  Calling tool: get_cow_definition with args: {'name': 'HasPeriod'}
[Orchestrator] Applying Patch...
```lean4
refine ⟨?_, ?_⟩
  · -- Show 0 < k * p
    have hp_pos := hp.1
    have hk_pos : 0 < k := by omega
    exact Nat.mul_pos hk_pos hp_pos
  · -- Show ∀ i, i + k * p < w.length → w.get? i = w.get? (i + k * p)
    intro i hi
    -- Induction on k
    match k, hk with
    | k'+1, _ =>
      clear hk
      induction k' with
      | zero =>
        -- k = 1, so k * p = p
        simp only [Nat.zero_add, Nat.one_mul] at hi ⊢
        exact hp.2 i hi
      | succ k' ih =>
        -- k = k' + 2, so k * p = (k' + 2) * p = (k' + 1) * p + p
        have hk'_ge : 1 ≤ k' + 1 := by omega
        have hi' : i + (k' + 1) * p < w.length := by
          calc i + (k' + 1) * p
              = i + (k' + 1) * p := rfl
            _ < i + (k' + 2) * p := by
                have : (k' + 1) * p < (k' + 2) * p := by
                  have hp_pos := hp.1
                  omega
                omega
            _ = i + k * p := by ring
            _ < w.length := hi
        have hi'' : i + (k' + 1) * p + p < w.length := by
          calc i + (k' + 1) * p + p
              = i + ((k' + 1) * p + p) := by omega
            _ = i + (k' + 2) * p := by ring
            _ = i + k * p := by ring
            _ < w.length := hi
        have step1 := ih hk'_ge hi'
        have step2 := hp.2 (i + (k' + 1) * p) hi''
        calc w.get? i
            = w.get? (i + (k' + 1) * p) := step1
          _ = w.get? (i + (k' + 1) * p + p) := step2
```

[PlannerAgent] Reasoning:
Looking at the Builder's attempt, I can see the structure is good but there are some issues with the arithmetic manipulations and the final `ring_nf` steps. The issue is likely that `ring_nf` or `ring` doesn't work directly on `get?` expressions in the expected way, or the arithmetic simplification isn't happening as expected.

Let me trace through what should happen:
- For `k = k' + 2`, we have `k * p = (k' + 2) * p`
- We want to show: `w.get? i = w.get? (i + (k' + 2) * p)`
- By IH: `w.get? i = w.get? (i + (k' + 1) * p)`
- By period: `w.get? (i + (k' + 1) * p) = w.get? (i + (k' + 1) * p + p)`
- Need to show: `i + (k' + 1) * p + p = i + (k' + 2) * p`

The issue is that in the calc chain, the Builder is using `ring_nf` on the entire `get?` expression, but we need to prove the arguments are equal, not apply ring normalization to the `get?` call itself.

The fix: use `congr_arg` or rewrite the index before applying to `get?`.

Another issue: the match statement might not be the cleanest way to handle this since we already know `k ≥ 1`.

Let me provide a cleaner strategy.

[PlannerAgent] Plan:
**Goal**: Prove `HasPeriod w (k * p)` given `HasPeriod w p` and `1 ≤ k`.

**Revised strategy** (fixing the Builder's arithmetic issues):

**Main issue**: The `ring_nf` tactic doesn't work on `w.get?` expressions. We need to prove index equality separately and then use `congr_arg` or rewrite.

**Step-by-step**:

1. **Structure**: Use `refine ⟨?pos, ?period⟩` to split into two goals

2. **Positivity** (`0 < k * p`):
   ```lean
   have hp_pos := hp.1
   have hk_pos : 0 < k := Nat.zero_lt_of_lt (Nat.one_le_iff_ne_zero.mp hk)
   exact Nat.mul_pos hk_pos hp_pos
   ```
   Or simply: `omega` after extracting the assumptions

3. **Periodicity** (fixed approach):
   
   Instead of using `match`, use `Nat.le_induction` or regular induction:
   
   ```lean
   intro i hi
   -- We'll prove this by induction on k
   -- Since k ≥ 1, we can use induction starting from 1
   ```
   
   **Better: Strong induction or direct induction on `k`**:
   
   Use the fact that we can prove it by induction with base case `k = 1`:
   
   - **Base case** `k = 1`:
     ```lean
     -- When k = 1: k * p = p
     -- Need: w.get? i = w.get? (i + p)
     -- This is exactly hp.2 i hi
     ```
   
   - **Inductive step**:
     For the inductive step, the key is to prove the index arithmetic separately:
     
     ```lean
     -- IH: For this particular i, if i + k' * p < w.length, 
     --     then w.get? i = w.get? (i + k' * p)
     -- Goal: w.get? i = w.get? (i + (k'+1) * p)
     
     -- First, establish arithmetic facts:
     have h_arith : i + (k'+1) * p = i + k' * p + p := by ring
     
     -- Rewrite the goal using this arithmetic fact:
     rw [h_arith]
     
     -- Now we can apply transitivity:
     -- w.get? i = w.get? (i + k' * p) (by IH)
     -- w.get? (i + k' * p) = w.get? (i + k' * p + p) (by hp.2)
     
     trans w.get? (i + k' * p)
     · apply ih  -- need to show i + k' * p < w.length
       <bound proof>
     · apply hp.2  -- need to show i + k' * p + p < w.length
       <bound proof>
     ```

4. **Cleaner implementation using `Nat.le_induction`**:

   Actually, the cleanest approach is to use `Nat.le_induction` on the hypothesis `hk : 1 ≤ k`:
   
   ```lean
   case period =>
     intro i hi
     induction k, hk using Nat.le_induction with
     | base =>  -- k = 1
       simp only [one_mul]
       exact hp.2 i hi
     | succ k' hk' ih =>  -- k' ≥ 1, proving for k' + 1
       have h_expand : (k' + 1) * p = k' * p + p := by ring
       calc w.get? i
           = w.get? (i + k' * p) := ih (by omega : i + k' * p < w.length)
         _ = w.get? (i + k' * p + p) := hp.2 (i + k' * p) (by omega)
         _ = w.get? (i + (k' + 1) * p) := by rw [← h_expand]; ring_nf
   ```

**Key fixes**:
1. Use `Nat.le_induction` which is perfect for this situation
2. Prove arithmetic facts separately before applying to `get?`
3. Use `trans` or `calc` for transitivity
4. Use `omega` for bound checking instead of manual calc chains
5. Only use `ring` on arithmetic expressions, not on `get?` applications

- `Nat.mul_pos` (from Mathlib)
